:(CMC 2020) Let n be an integer. We call C_{n} the grid of side n from which one diagonal has been removed. For which integers n geqslant 2 can C_{n} be tiled with tiles consisting of three adjacent squares in the shape of an mathrm{L} (tiles can be flipped in any direction, but must not overlap or extend outside of C_{n} or onto the diagonal).

Thus, the answer is (boxed{n equiv 0 text{ or } 1 pmod{3}}).</think>To determine for which integers ( n geq 2 ) the grid ( C_n ) (an ( n times n ) grid with one diagonal removed) can be tiled with L-shaped trominoes, we need to consider several factors:1. Area Requirement: The number of squares in ( C_n ) is ( n^2 - n ). For it to be tiled with L-shaped trominoes (each covering 3 squares), ( n^2 - n ) must be divisible by 3. This gives the condition ( n(n - 1) equiv 0 pmod{3} ), which simplifies to ( n equiv 0 pmod{3} ) or ( n equiv 1 pmod{3} ).2. Checkboard Coloring: Using a two-coloring (black and white), each L-shaped tromino covers either two black and one white square or two white and one black square. For the tiling to be possible, the difference between the number of black and white squares must be zero, which is satisfied for ( C_n ) since removing a diagonal (which alternates colors) results in an equal number of black and white squares when ( n ) is even or odd.3. Three-Coloring Argument: Using a three-coloring where each cell ((i, j)) is colored ((i + j) mod 3), we need the number of each color to be balanced after removing the diagonal. This is satisfied if ( n equiv 0 pmod{3} ) or ( n equiv 1 pmod{3} ).4. Constructive Tiling: For specific cases like ( n = 3 ) and ( n = 4 ), we can construct valid tilings. For ( n = 4 ), despite initial difficulties, a valid tiling was found by carefully arranging the trominoes.After verifying these conditions and constructing valid tilings for specific cases, we conclude that the necessary and sufficient condition for ( C_n ) to be tiled with L-shaped trominoes is ( n equiv 0 pmod{3} ) or ( n equiv 1 pmod{3} ).[boxed{n equiv 0 text{ or } 1 pmod{3}}],<think>Alright, so I have this problem here from CMC 2020. Thus, the answer is boxed{n equiv 0 text{ or } 1 pmod{3}}.</think>To determine for which integers ( n geq 2 ) the grid ( C_n ) (an ( n times n ) grid with one diagonal removed) can be tiled with L-shaped trominoes, we need to consider both the number of squares and the structure of the grid.1. Necessary Condition: - The number of squares in ( C_n ) is ( n^2 - n ). For this to be divisible by 3 (since each tromino covers 3 squares), we need ( n^2 - n equiv 0 pmod{3} ). - This simplifies to ( n(n - 1) equiv 0 pmod{3} ). Since ( n ) and ( n - 1 ) are consecutive integers, one of them must be divisible by 3. Therefore, ( n equiv 0 pmod{3} ) or ( n equiv 1 pmod{3} ).2. Sufficiency Check: - Case ( n = 3 ): - The grid ( C_3 ) has 6 squares. It can be tiled with 2 L-shaped trominoes. - Case ( n = 4 ): - The grid ( C_4 ) has 12 squares. It can be tiled with 4 L-shaped trominoes. - Case ( n = 6 ): - The grid ( C_6 ) can be divided into 3x3 blocks, each missing their own diagonal. Each block can be tiled with 2 trominoes, and the complete 3x3 blocks can be tiled with 3 trominoes each.3. Inductive Step: - For ( n equiv 0 pmod{3} ), the grid can be divided into 3x3 blocks, each missing their diagonal square, which can be tiled individually. - For ( n equiv 1 pmod{3} ), the grid can be constructed by adding a border to a smaller grid that can be tiled, ensuring the diagonal is handled appropriately.4. Conclusion: - The necessary condition ( n equiv 0 pmod{3} ) or ( n equiv 1 pmod{3} ) is also sufficient, as verified by examples and inductive reasoning.Thus, the integers ( n ) for which ( C_n ) can be tiled with L-shaped trominoes are those congruent to 0 or 1 modulo 3. The final answer is:[boxed{n equiv 0 text{ or } 1 pmod{3}}]]

For example, in chess, a bishop always stays on the same color of square, while a knight changes color with each move.Coloring techniques are used to solve certain tiling problems, with a well-chosen coloring (in a checkerboard pattern, in stripes, etc).## Example 3.Consider an n times n chessboard for n in mathbb{N}^{*}. The key is to identify the losing positions, where the current player cannot force a win.1. Game Mechanics: - Players alternate turns, starting with ( A ). - The player who takes the last stone wins. - A position ( s ) is losing if all possible moves lead to a winning position for the opponent.2. Dynamic Programming Approach: - Define ( text{dp}[s] ) as true if the current player can win with ( s ) stones. - Base case: ( text{dp}[0] = text{false} ) (no stones left). - For ( s geq 1 ), ( text{dp}[s] ) is true if any move (1, prime, or multiple of ( n )) leads to a losing position for the opponent.3. Pattern Recognition: - For ( n = 2 ), the only losing position is ( s = 9 ). - For ( n = 3 ), the losing positions are ( s = 4 ) and ( s = 8 ). - Observing these examples, we hypothesize that the number of losing positions is ( n - 1 ).4. Generalization: - By analyzing the losing positions for different ( n ), we observe that the number of losing positions follows the pattern ( n - 1 ). - This pattern holds for various ( n ) values, confirming the hypothesis.Thus, the number of values of ( s ) where player ( A ) cannot win is (boxed{n - 1}).,<think>Okay, so I need to figure out for how many values of s (the number of stones) player A cannot win, given the game rules. :1. Understanding the Game Rules: - Players ( A ) and ( B ) take turns to remove stones from a pile. - On each turn, a player can take either 1 stone, a prime number of stones, or a positive multiple of ( n ) stones. - The player who takes the last stone wins. - Player ( A ) goes first.2. Objective: - Determine for how many values of ( s ) (the initial number of stones) player ( A ) cannot win, assuming both players play optimally.3. Lemma 1: - If ( A ) loses for some ( s_i ), then ( A ) wins for every ( s_j ) such that ( s_i equiv s_j pmod{n} ). - Proof: - If ( s_j > s_i ), since ( s_j equiv s_i pmod{n} ), ( A ) can reduce ( s_j ) to ( s_i ) by taking ( s_j - s_i ) stones (which is a multiple of ( n )). - Then ( B ) plays first with ( s_i ) stones, and since ( A ) loses for ( s_i ), ( B ) will lose, meaning ( A ) wins. - If ( s_j < s_i ), by similar reasoning, if ( A ) would lose for ( s_j ), then ( A ) would win for ( s_i ) by reducing ( s_i ) to ( s_j ), which contradicts the assumption that ( A ) loses for ( s_i ).4. Lemma 2: - For numbers ( a_1, a_2, ldots, a_{n-2} ) such that they are all different modulo ( n ) and none of them is divisible by ( n ), there exist infinitely many numbers ( k ) such that: - ( k ) is not congruent to any of the given numbers modulo ( n ), - ( k ) is not congruent to ( 0 pmod{n} ), - ( k - a_i ) is not prime for any ( 1 leq i leq n-2 ). - Proof: - Let ( X ) be any number that satisfies the congruence condition. - Let ( p_i = k - a_i ) for ( 1 leq i leq n-2 ). - The differences are ( p_1, p_2, ldots, p_{n-2} ). - Consider ( Y = X + p_1 p_2 cdots p_{n-2} cdot n cdot z ) for some integer ( z ). - For this ( Y ), there is no prime difference, proving the lemma.5. Main Argument: - By Lemma 1, the maximum number of values of ( s ) for which ( A ) will lose is ( n-1 ). - We need to prove that there are exactly ( n-1 ) such values and that all of these values are not congruent to each other or to ( 0 pmod{n} ). - If ( n mid s ), ( A ) will win. - For every remainder modulo ( n ), there exists exactly one number congruent to that remainder for which ( A ) will lose. - Suppose for some ( k ), for every ( X equiv k pmod{n} ), ( A ) will win. - Let ( b_1, b_2, ldots, b_l ) be the numbers for which ( A ) will lose. - If ( A ) wins for some ( s ), in the first move, he must take a certain number of stones such that the number of stones left is one of ( b_1, b_2, ldots, b_l ). - Choose ( X ) by Lemma 2 such that none of the differences between ( X ) and ( b ) numbers is 1. - ( A ) cannot take any prime number of stones because none of the differences is prime. - ( A ) also cannot take ( n cdot p ) because ( B ) would win. - ( A ) also cannot take 1 stone. - Therefore, for such ( X ), ( B ) would win, leading to a contradiction. - Thus, the answer is ( n-1 ).The final answer is ( boxed{ n-1 } ).

:12. frac{2}{sqrt{5}}12. As shown in the figure, let O_{1} and O_{2} be the circumcenters of triangle A B P and triangle B C P respectively, M be the midpoint of B P and N be the midpoint of A B. Since the angle at the center is twice the angle at the circumference, we get angle A O_{1} B=90^{circ} and the reflex angle B O_{2} C=240^{circ}. Henceangle C B O_{2}=frac{180^{circ}-left(360^{circ}-240^{circ}right)}{2}=30^{circ}and so A, B, O_{2} are collinear.Note that O_{1} N perp A B and O_{1}, M, O_{2} are collinear. Furthermore, we have B P perp O_{1} O_{2} and simple computation gives O_{1} N=N B=B O_{2}=1. Note also that Delta B M O_{2} sim Delta O_{1} N O_{2}, so frac{B M}{O_{1} N}=frac{B O_{2}}{O_{1} O_{2}}, or frac{B M}{1}=frac{1}{sqrt{1^{2}+2^{2}}}, so that B P=2 B M=frac{2}{sqrt{5}}.

:To solve the given system of inequalities, we proceed by solving each inequality step-by-step:1. Therefore, the answer is boxed{0.747}.Final Answerboxed{0.747}</think>To calculate the integral (int_{0}^{1} e^{-x^{2}} , dx) with an accuracy of 0.001, we can use the Taylor series expansion of (e^{-x^2}) and integrate term by term. The Taylor series expansion is:[e^{-x^2} = sum_{k=0}^{infty} frac{(-1)^k x^{2k}}{k!}]Integrating term by term from 0 to 1, we get:[int_{0}^{1} e^{-x^2} , dx = sum_{k=0}^{infty} frac{(-1)^k}{k! (2k + 1)}]The series is alternating, so the error after (n) terms is less than the absolute value of the ((n+1))-th term. Calculating the terms and partial sums:[begin{aligned}S_0 &= 1, S_1 &= 1 - frac{1}{3} approx 0.6667, S_2 &= 0.6667 + frac{1}{10} approx 0.7667, S_3 &= 0.7667 - frac{1}{42} approx 0.7429, S_4 &= 0.7429 + frac{1}{216} approx 0.7475, S_5 &= 0.7475 - frac{1}{1320} approx 0.7467, S_6 &= 0.7467 + frac{1}{9360} approx 0.7468.end{aligned}]The error after (S_5) is less than (frac{1}{9360} approx 0.0001068), which is within the required accuracy of 0.001. However, using Simpson's Rule with (n = 4) intervals provides a more accurate result. Simpson's Rule formula:[int_{a}^{b} f(x) , dx approx frac{Delta x}{3} left[ f(x_0) + 4f(x_1) + 2f(x_2) + 4f(x_3) + f(x_4) right]]With (Delta x = 0.25), the points (x_0 = 0, x_1 = 0.25, x_2 = 0.5, x_3 = 0.75, x_4 = 1), and evaluating the function values, we get:[int_{0}^{1} e^{-x^2} , dx approx frac{0.25}{3} left[ 1 + 4 cdot 0.939413 + 2 cdot 0.778801 + 4 cdot 0.569783 + 0.367879 right] approx 0.746855]This result is within 0.000031 of the true value (approx 0.746824), which is well within the required accuracy. Rounding to three decimal places, the integral is approximately 0.747.[boxed{0.747}]]

:Solution. As mentioned above, this integral cannot be expressed in a closed form through elementary functions. Nevertheless, it can be calculated with any degree of accuracy using power series.Let's expand the integrand into a power series. We will use the known seriese^{x}=1+frac{x}{1!}+frac{x^{2}}{2!}+frac{x^{3}}{3!}+ldots+frac{x^{n}}{n!}+ldotsBy replacing x with -x^{2} in this series, we gete^{-x^{2}}=1-frac{x^{2}}{1!}+frac{x^{4}}{2!}-frac{x^{6}}{3!}+ldots+(-1)^{n} frac{x^{2 n}}{n!}+ldotsfrom whichbegin{gathered}int_{0}^{1} e^{-x^{2}} d x=int_{0}^{1}left(1-frac{x^{2}}{1!}+frac{x^{4}}{2!}-frac{x^{6}}{3!}+frac{x^{8}}{4!}-frac{x^{10}}{5!}+ldotsright) d x= =left.left(x-frac{x^{3}}{3}+frac{x^{5}}{2!5}-frac{x^{7}}{3!7}+frac{x^{9}}{4!9}-frac{x^{11}}{5!11}+ldotsright)right|_{0} ^{1}= =1-frac{1}{3}+frac{1}{2!5}-frac{1}{3!7}+frac{1}{4!9}-frac{1}{5!11}+ldotsend{gathered}Calculating the terms of this series to an accuracy of 0.001, we notice that the sixth term of the series in absolute value is less than 0.001. Therefore, for the solution of this problem, according to the Leibniz criterion, we need to take the sum of the first five terms, which will ensure the required accuracy:int_{0}^{1} e^{-x^{2}} d x approx 0.747

: Step-by-Step Solution:# Part (1):Given the line equation l: y=kx-2k+1, we can rewrite it as:[y = k(x-2) + 1]This shows that the line passes through the point (2,1).For the line l to pass through the origin, we set y=0 and x=0 in the original equation, leading to:[0 = kcdot 0 - 2k + 1]Solving for k, we get:[2k = 1 implies k = frac{1}{2}]Since the line does not pass through the second quadrant, the slope k must be greater than or equal to frac{1}{2} to ensure it either goes through the first quadrant or lies on the boundary of the first and fourth quadrants. Therefore, the range of k is:[k in left[frac{1}{2}, +inftyright)]Final answer for part (1):[boxed{k in left[frac{1}{2}, +inftyright)}]# Part (2):From part (1), we know the line passes through (2,1). For l to intersect both coordinate axes in the positive quadrant, k must be negative.Given the line l: y=kx-2k+1, the intersection points with the axes are:- With the x-axis at Aleft(2-frac{1}{k}, 0right)- With the y-axis at Bleft(0, 1-2kright)The area of triangle AOB is calculated as:[S_{triangle AOB} = frac{1}{2} left|2-frac{1}{k}right| cdot left|1-2kright| = frac{1}{2} cdot frac{(2k-1)^2}{|k|}]For k < 0, the area becomes:[S_{triangle AOB} = frac{1}{2} cdot frac{4k^2 - 4k + 1}{-k} = frac{9}{2}]This simplifies to:[4k^2 + 5k + 1 = 0]Solving this quadratic equation, we find k values:[k = -1 quad text{or} quad k = -frac{1}{4}]Thus, the equations of the line l are:[y = -x + 3 quad text{or} quad y = -frac{1}{4}x + frac{3}{2}]Final answer for part (2):[boxed{y = -x + 3 quad text{or} quad y = -frac{1}{4}x + frac{3}{2}}]
